<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Agriculture</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"><div class="flaggedrevs_short flaggedrevs_stable_synced plainlinks noprint nomobile" id="mw-fr-revisiontag"><div class="flaggedrevs_short_basic"><img alt="Changes must be reviewed before being displayed on this page." class="flaggedrevs-icon" src="/w/extensions/FlaggedRevs/frontend/modules/img/doc-magnify.png" title="Changes must be reviewed before being displayed on this page."/><img alt="show/hide details" class="fr-toggle-arrow" id="mw-fr-revisiontoggle" src="/w/extensions/FlaggedRevs/frontend/modules/img/arrow-down.png" style="display:none;"/></div>
<div id="mw-fr-revisiondetails-wrapper" style="position:relative;"><div class="flaggedrevs_short_details" id="mw-fr-revisiondetails" style="display:none">This is the <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Pending_changes" title="Wikipedia:Pending changes">latest accepted revision</a>, <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Special:Log&amp;type=review&amp;page=Agriculture">reviewed</a> on <i>16 February 2019</i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Farming" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Farming_(disambiguation)" title="Farming (disambiguation)">Farming (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Cultivation of plants and animals to provide useful products</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:482px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Unload_wheat_by_the_combine_Claas_Lexion_584.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1082" data-file-width="2450" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Unload_wheat_by_the_combine_Claas_Lexion_584.jpg/480px-Unload_wheat_by_the_combine_Claas_Lexion_584.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Unload_wheat_by_the_combine_Claas_Lexion_584.jpg/720px-Unload_wheat_by_the_combine_Claas_Lexion_584.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Unload_wheat_by_the_combine_Claas_Lexion_584.jpg/960px-Unload_wheat_by_the_combine_Claas_Lexion_584.jpg 2x" width="480"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Unload_wheat_by_the_combine_Claas_Lexion_584.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Harvest" title="Harvest">Harvesting</a> <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Combine_harvester" title="Combine harvester">combine harvester</a> accompanied by a tractor and trailer</div></div></div>
<p><b>Agriculture</b> is the science and art of cultivating plants and livestock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Office1999_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Office1999-1">[1]</a></sup> Agriculture was the key development in the rise of <a href="/wiki/Sedentism" title="Sedentism">sedentary</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_civilization" title="Human civilization">human civilization</a>, whereby farming of <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">domesticated</a> species created food <a href="/wiki/Economic_surplus" title="Economic surplus">surpluses</a> that enabled people to live in cities. The <a href="/wiki/History_of_agriculture" title="History of agriculture">history of agriculture</a> began thousands of years ago. After gathering wild grains beginning at least 105,000 years ago, nascent farmers began to plant them around 11,500 years ago. Pigs, sheep and cattle were domesticated over 10,000 years ago. Plants were independently cultivated in at least 11 regions of the world. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrial_agriculture" title="Industrial agriculture">Industrial agriculture</a> based on large-scale <a href="/wiki/Monoculture" title="Monoculture">monoculture</a> in the twentieth century came to dominate agricultural output, though about 2 billion people still depended on <a href="/wiki/Subsistence_agriculture" title="Subsistence agriculture">subsistence agriculture</a> into the twenty-first.
</p><p>Modern <a href="/wiki/Agronomy" title="Agronomy">agronomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plant_breeding" title="Plant breeding">plant breeding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agrochemical" title="Agrochemical">agrochemicals</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizers</a>, and technological developments have sharply increased yields, while causing widespread ecological and environmental damage. <a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">Selective breeding</a> and modern practices in <a href="/wiki/Animal_husbandry" title="Animal husbandry">animal husbandry</a> have similarly increased the output of meat, but have raised concerns about <a href="/wiki/Animal_welfare" title="Animal welfare">animal welfare</a> and environmental damage. Environmental issues include contributions to <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>, depletion of <a href="/wiki/Aquifer" title="Aquifer">aquifers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibiotic_resistance" title="Antibiotic resistance">antibiotic resistance</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Growth_hormone" title="Growth hormone">growth hormones</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrial_meat_production" title="Industrial meat production">industrial meat production</a>. <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_organism" title="Genetically modified organism">Genetically modified organisms</a> are widely used, although they are banned in some countries.
</p><p>The major agricultural products can be broadly grouped into foods, fibers, <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Raw_material" title="Raw material">raw materials</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Natural_rubber" title="Natural rubber">rubber</a>). Food crop classes include <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">cereals</a> (grains), <a href="/wiki/Vegetable" title="Vegetable">vegetables</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">fruits</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cooking_oil" title="Cooking oil">oils</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meat" title="Meat">meat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Milk" title="Milk">milk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edible_mushroom" title="Edible mushroom">fungi</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eggs_as_food" title="Eggs as food">eggs</a>. Over one-third of the world's workers are employed in agriculture, second only to the service sector, although the number of agricultural workers in developed countries has decreased significantly over the centuries.
</p>

<li><a href="/wiki/Category:Poultry_farming" title="Category:Poultry farming">Poultry farming</a></li></ul>
</div></td>
</tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold">
<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Veranotrigo.jpg"><img alt="Veranotrigo.jpg" class="noviewer" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Veranotrigo.jpg/16px-Veranotrigo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Veranotrigo.jpg/24px-Veranotrigo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Veranotrigo.jpg/32px-Veranotrigo.jpg 2x" width="16"/></a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Portal:Agriculture_and_agronomy" title="Portal:Agriculture and agronomy">Agriculture and agronomy portal</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Agriculture" title="Template:Agriculture"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Agriculture" title="Template talk:Agriculture"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Agriculture&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_scope">Etymology and scope</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology and scope">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The word <i>agriculture</i> is a late <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> adaptation of Latin <i>agricultūra</i>, from <i>ager</i>, "field", which in its turn came from <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> αγρός, and <i>cultūra</i>, "<a href="/wiki/Tillage" title="Tillage">cultivation</a>" or "growing".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> While agriculture usually refers to human activities, certain species of <a href="/wiki/Ant" title="Ant">ant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Termite" title="Termite">termite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ambrosia_beetle" title="Ambrosia beetle">ambrosia beetle</a> also cultivate crops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Agriculture is defined with varying scopes, in its broadest sense using natural resources to "produce commodities which maintain life, including food, fiber, forest products, horticultural crops, and their related services".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maine_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maine-4">[4]</a></sup> Thus defined, it includes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arable_farming" title="Arable farming">arable farming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horticulture" title="Horticulture">horticulture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Animal_husbandry" title="Animal husbandry">animal husbandry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Forestry" title="Forestry">forestry</a>, but horticulture and forestry are in practice often excluded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maine_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Maine-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_agriculture" title="History of agriculture">History of agriculture</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">Neolithic Revolution</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vavilov-centers_updated.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="614" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Vavilov-centers_updated.jpg/300px-Vavilov-centers_updated.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Vavilov-centers_updated.jpg/450px-Vavilov-centers_updated.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Vavilov-centers_updated.jpg/600px-Vavilov-centers_updated.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vavilov-centers_updated.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Center_of_origin" title="Center of origin">Centres of origin</a>, as numbered by <a href="/wiki/Nikolai_Vavilov" title="Nikolai Vavilov">Nikolai Vavilov</a> in the 1930s. Area 3 (gray) is no longer recognised as a centre of origin, and <a href="/wiki/Papua_New_Guinea" title="Papua New Guinea">Papua New Guinea</a> (area P, orange) was identified more recently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Larson2014_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Larson2014-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The development of agriculture enabled the human population to grow many times larger than could be sustained by <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunting and gathering</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bocquet-Appel_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bocquet-Appel-7">[7]</a></sup> Agriculture began independently in different parts of the globe, and included a diverse range of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taxa" title="Taxa">taxa</a>. At least 11 separate regions independently began the practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Larson2014_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Larson2014-5">[5]</a></sup> Wild grains were collected and eaten from at least 105,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> From around 11,500 years ago, the eight <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_founder_crops" title="Neolithic founder crops">Neolithic founder crops</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emmer_wheat" title="Emmer wheat">emmer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Einkorn_wheat" title="Einkorn wheat">einkorn wheat</a>, hulled <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pea" title="Pea">peas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lentil" title="Lentil">lentils</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vicia_ervilia" title="Vicia ervilia">bitter vetch</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chick_pea" title="Chick pea">chick peas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flax" title="Flax">flax</a> were cultivated in the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>. <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">Rice</a> was domesticated in China between 11,500 and 6,200 BC with the earliest known cultivation from 5,700 BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> followed by <a href="/wiki/Mung_bean" title="Mung bean">mung</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soy" title="Soy">soy</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Azuki_bean" title="Azuki bean">azuki</a> beans. <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">Sheep</a> were domesticated in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> between 13,000 and 11,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">Cattle</a> were domesticated from the wild <a href="/wiki/Aurochs" title="Aurochs">aurochs</a> in the areas of modern Turkey and Pakistan some 10,500 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McTavish_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-McTavish-11">[11]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Domestic_pig" title="Domestic pig">Pig production</a> emerged in Eurasia, including Europe, East Asia and Southwest Asia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> where <a href="/wiki/Wild_boar" title="Wild boar">wild boar</a> were first domesticated about 10,500 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">Andes</a> of <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">potato</a> was domesticated between 10,000 and 7,000 years ago, along with beans, <a href="/wiki/Coca" title="Coca">coca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Llama" title="Llama">llamas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alpaca" title="Alpaca">alpacas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Guinea_pig" title="Guinea pig">guinea pigs</a>. <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">Sugarcane</a> and some <a href="/wiki/List_of_root_vegetables" title="List of root vegetables">root vegetables</a> were domesticated in <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a> around 9,000 years ago. <a href="/wiki/Sorghum" title="Sorghum">Sorghum</a> was domesticated in the <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahel</a> region of Africa by 7,000 years ago. <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">Cotton</a> was domesticated in <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a> by 5,600 years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Broudy1979_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Broudy1979-14">[14]</a></sup> and was independently domesticated in Eurasia. <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_Mesoamerica" title="Agriculture in Mesoamerica">In Mesoamerica</a>, wild <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teosinte" title="Teosinte">teosinte</a> was bred into <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a> by 6,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
Scholars have offered multiple hypotheses to explain the historical origins of agriculture. Studies of the transition from <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherer</a> to agricultural societies indicate an initial period of intensification and increasing <a href="/wiki/Sedentism" title="Sedentism">sedentism</a>; examples are the <a href="/wiki/Natufian_culture" title="Natufian culture">Natufian culture</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, and the Early Chinese Neolithic in China. Then, wild stands that had previously been harvested started to be planted, and gradually came to be domesticated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civilizations">Civilizations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Civilizations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tomb_of_Nakht_(2).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="828" data-file-width="630" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Tomb_of_Nakht_%282%29.jpg/170px-Tomb_of_Nakht_%282%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Tomb_of_Nakht_%282%29.jpg/255px-Tomb_of_Nakht_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Tomb_of_Nakht_%282%29.jpg/340px-Tomb_of_Nakht_%282%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tomb_of_Nakht_(2).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Agricultural scenes of <a href="/wiki/Threshing" title="Threshing">threshing</a>, a grain store, harvesting with <a href="/wiki/Sickle" title="Sickle">sickles</a>, digging, tree-cutting and ploughing from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_agriculture" title="Ancient Egyptian agriculture">Ancient Egypt</a>. Tomb of <a href="/wiki/Nakht" title="Nakht">Nakht</a>, 15th century BC</div></div></div>
<p>In Eurasia, the <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerians</a> started to build villages from about 8,000 BC, relying on the <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a> rivers and a canal system for irrigation. Ploughs appear in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pictograph" title="Pictograph">pictographs</a> around 3,000 BC; seed-ploughs around 2,300 BC. Farmers grew wheat, barley, vegetables such as lentils and onions, and fruits including dates, grapes, and figs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritMus_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-BritMus-18">[18]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_agriculture" title="Ancient Egyptian agriculture">Ancient Egyptian agriculture</a> relied on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nile_River" title="Nile River">Nile River</a> and its seasonal flooding. Farming started in the predynastic period at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a>, after 10,000 BC. Staple food crops were grains such as wheat and barley, alongside industrial crops such as <a href="/wiki/Flax" title="Flax">flax</a> and <a href="/wiki/Papyrus" title="Papyrus">papyrus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Janick_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Janick-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_India" title="Agriculture in India">India</a>, wheat, barley and <a href="/wiki/Jujube" title="Jujube">jujube</a> were domesticated by 9,000 BC, soon followed by sheep and goats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gupta_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-gupta-21">[21]</a></sup> Cattle, sheep and goats were domesticated in <a href="/wiki/Mehrgarh" title="Mehrgarh">Mehrgarh culture</a> by 8,000–6,000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baber_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baber-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harrisandgosden385_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-harrisandgosden385-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harrisandgosden385_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-harrisandgosden385-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Possehl_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Possehl-24">[24]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">Cotton</a> was cultivated by the 5th-4th millennium BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Archeological evidence indicates an animal-drawn <a href="/wiki/Plough" title="Plough">plough</a> from 2,500 BC in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley" title="Indus Valley">Indus Valley</a> civilization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lal_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-lal-26">[26]</a></sup>
In <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_China" title="Agriculture in China">China</a>, from the 5th century BC a nationwide <a href="/wiki/Granary" title="Granary">granary</a> system accompanied widespread <a href="/wiki/Sericulture" title="Sericulture">silk farming</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_volume_6_part_2_55_56_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_volume_6_part_2_55_56-27">[27]</a></sup> Water-powered grain mills were in use by the 1st century BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_volume_4_part_2_184_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_volume_4_part_2_184-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_volume_4_part_2_89_110_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_volume_4_part_2_89_110-29">[29]</a></sup> followed by irrigation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_volume_4_part_2_110_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_volume_4_part_2_110-30">[30]</a></sup> By the late 2nd century, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heavy_plough" title="Heavy plough">heavy ploughs</a> had been developed with iron ploughshares and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mouldboard" title="Mouldboard">mouldboards</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greenberger_2006_11-12_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-greenberger_2006_11-12-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> These spread westwards across Eurasia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Asian rice was domesticated 8,200–13,500 years ago – depending on the <a href="/wiki/Molecular_clock" title="Molecular clock">molecular clock</a> estimate that is used<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pnas1_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-pnas1-34">[34]</a></sup> – on the Pearl River in southern China with a single genetic origin from the wild rice <i><a href="/wiki/Oryza_rufipogon" title="Oryza rufipogon">Oryza rufipogon</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature1_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature1-35">[35]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_ancient_Greece" title="Agriculture in ancient Greece">Greece</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_agriculture" title="Roman agriculture">Rome</a>, the major cereals were wheat, emmer, and barley, alongside vegetables including peas, beans, and olives. Sheep and goats were kept mainly for dairy products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-koester_1995_p76-77_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-koester_1995_p76-77-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-White_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-White-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the Americas, crops domesticated in Mesoamerica (apart from teosinte) include squash, beans, and cocoa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murphy2011_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Murphy2011-38">[38]</a></sup> Cocoa was being domesticated by the Mayo Chinchipe of the upper Amazon around 3,000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
The <a href="/wiki/Domestic_turkey" title="Domestic turkey">turkey</a> was probably domesticated in Mexico or the American Southwest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Speller_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Speller-40">[40]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aztec" title="Aztec">Aztecs</a> developed irrigation systems, formed <a href="/wiki/Terrace_(agriculture)" title="Terrace (agriculture)">terraced</a> hillsides, fertilized their soil, and developed <a href="/wiki/Chinampa" title="Chinampa">chinampas</a> or artificial islands. The <a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization" title="Maya civilization">Mayas</a> used extensive canal and raised field systems to farm swampland from 400 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spooner_2005_14694–99_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spooner_2005_14694–99-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-online_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-online-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John_Michael_Francis_2005_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-John_Michael_Francis_2005-45">[45]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Coca" title="Coca">Coca</a> was domesticated in the Andes, as were the <a href="/wiki/Peanut" title="Peanut">peanut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tomato" title="Tomato">tomato</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tobacco" title="Tobacco">tobacco</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pineapple" title="Pineapple">pineapple</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murphy2011_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Murphy2011-38">[38]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">Cotton</a> was domesticated in <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a> by 3,600 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Broudy1979_p81_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Broudy1979_p81-46">[46]</a></sup> Animals including <a href="/wiki/Llama" title="Llama">llamas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alpaca" title="Alpaca">alpacas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Guinea_pig" title="Guinea pig">guinea pigs</a> were domesticated there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RischkowskyPilling2007_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-RischkowskyPilling2007-47">[47]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/History_of_agriculture_in_the_United_States" title="History of agriculture in the United States">North America</a>, the indigenous people of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Agricultural_Complex" title="Eastern Agricultural Complex">East domesticated crops</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunflower" title="Sunflower">sunflower</a>, tobacco,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> squash and <i><a href="/wiki/Chenopodium" title="Chenopodium">Chenopodium</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Wild foods including <a href="/wiki/Wild_rice" title="Wild rice">wild rice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maple_sugar" title="Maple sugar">maple sugar</a> were harvested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith2013_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith2013-51">[51]</a></sup> The domesticated <a href="/wiki/Strawberry" title="Strawberry">strawberry</a> is a hybrid of a Chilean and a North American species, developed by breeding in Europe and North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_the_prehistoric_Southwest" title="Agriculture in the prehistoric Southwest">indigenous people of the Southwest</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Northwest" title="Pacific Northwest">Pacific Northwest</a> practiced <a href="/wiki/Forest_gardening" title="Forest gardening">forest gardening</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fire-stick_farming" title="Fire-stick farming">fire-stick farming</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Native_American_use_of_fire" title="Native American use of fire">natives controlled fire</a> on a regional scale to create a low-intensity <a href="/wiki/Fire_ecology" title="Fire ecology">fire ecology</a> that <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_agriculture" title="Sustainable agriculture">sustained a low-density agriculture</a> in loose rotation; a sort of "wild" <a href="/wiki/Permaculture" title="Permaculture">permaculture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cunningham2010_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cunningham2010-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> A system of <a href="/wiki/Companion_planting" title="Companion planting">companion planting</a> called <a href="/wiki/Three_Sisters_(agriculture)" title="Three Sisters (agriculture)">the Three Sisters</a> was <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_on_the_prehistoric_Great_Plains" title="Agriculture on the prehistoric Great Plains">developed on the Great Plains</a>. The three crops were <a href="/wiki/Winter_squash" title="Winter squash">winter squash</a>, maize, and climbing beans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilson_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-wilson-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-landon_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-landon-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Indigenous_Australians" title="Indigenous Australians">Indigenous Australians</a>, long supposed to have been nomadic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherers" title="Hunter-gatherers">hunter-gatherers</a>, practised systematic burning to enhance natural productivity in fire-stick farming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gunditjmara" title="Gunditjmara">Gunditjmara</a> and other groups developed eel farming and fish trapping systems from some 5,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> There is evidence of 'intensification' across the whole continent over that period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> In two regions of Australia, the central west coast and eastern central, early farmers may have cultivated yams, native millet, and bush onions in permanent settlements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Revolution">Revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Islamic_Spain_agricultural_scene.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="713" data-file-width="625" decoding="async" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Islamic_Spain_agricultural_scene.jpg/200px-Islamic_Spain_agricultural_scene.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Islamic_Spain_agricultural_scene.jpg/300px-Islamic_Spain_agricultural_scene.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Islamic_Spain_agricultural_scene.jpg/400px-Islamic_Spain_agricultural_scene.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Islamic_Spain_agricultural_scene.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Arab_Agricultural_Revolution" title="Arab Agricultural Revolution">Arab Agricultural Revolution</a>, starting in <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Al-Andalus</a> (Islamic Spain), transformed agriculture with improved techniques and the diffusion of crop plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-64">[64]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In the Middle Ages, both <a href="/wiki/Arab_Agricultural_Revolution" title="Arab Agricultural Revolution">in the Islamic world</a> and in Europe, agriculture improved techniques and spread crop specimens throughout the region, including sugar, rice, cotton and fruit trees such as the <a href="/wiki/Orange_(fruit)" title="Orange (fruit)">orange</a> to Europe by way of <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Al-Andalus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NatGeographic2015_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-NatGeographic2015-65">[65]</a></sup> After 1492, the <a href="/wiki/Columbian_exchange" title="Columbian exchange">Columbian exchange</a> brought New World crops such as maize, potatoes, tomatoes, <a href="/wiki/Sweet_potato" title="Sweet potato">sweet potatoes</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manioc" title="Manioc">manioc</a> to Europe, and Old World crops such as wheat, barley, rice and <a href="/wiki/Turnip" title="Turnip">turnips</a>, and livestock including horses, cattle, sheep and goats to the Americas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">Irrigation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">crop rotation</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertilizers" title="Fertilizers">fertilizers</a> advanced beginning in the 19th century, triggered by the <a href="/wiki/British_Agricultural_Revolution" title="British Agricultural Revolution">British Agricultural Revolution</a>, allowing global population to rise significantly. Since 1900, agriculture in developed nations, and to a lesser extent in the developing world, has seen explosive productivity rises as human labor was replaced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mechanized_farming" title="Mechanized farming">mechanization</a>, and assisted by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synthetic_fertilizer" title="Synthetic fertilizer">synthetic fertilizers</a>, pesticides, and <a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">selective breeding</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haber-Bosch" title="Haber-Bosch">Haber-Bosch</a> method allowed the synthesis of <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_nitrate" title="Ammonium nitrate">ammonium nitrate</a> fertilizer on an industrial scale, greatly increasing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crop_yields" title="Crop yields">crop yields</a> and sustaining a further increase in global population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Modern agriculture raised issues including <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">water pollution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_organism" title="Genetically modified organism">genetically modified organisms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tariff" title="Tariff">tariffs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_subsidy" title="Agricultural subsidy">farm subsidies</a>, leading to alternative approaches such as the <a href="/wiki/Organic_movement" title="Organic movement">organic movement</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-motherjones1_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-motherjones1-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smh.com.au_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-smh.com.au-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Reindeer_herding.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Reindeer_herding.jpg/220px-Reindeer_herding.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Reindeer_herding.jpg/330px-Reindeer_herding.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Reindeer_herding.jpg/440px-Reindeer_herding.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Reindeer_herding.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Reindeer" title="Reindeer">Reindeer</a> herds form the basis of pastoral agriculture for several Arctic and Subarctic peoples.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pastoralism" title="Pastoralism">Pastoralism</a> involves managing domesticated animals. In <a href="/wiki/Nomadic_pastoralism" title="Nomadic pastoralism">nomadic pastoralism</a>, herds of livestock are moved from place to place in search of pasture, fodder, and water. This type of farming is practised in arid and semi-arid regions of <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a> and some parts of India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Shifting_cultivation" title="Shifting cultivation">shifting cultivation</a>, forest area is cleared by harvesting/burning the trees. The land is then used for growing crops until the soil becomes less fertile, when the area is abandoned. Another patch of land is selected and the process is repeated. This type of farming is practiced mainly in areas with abundant rainfall where the forest regenerates quickly. This practice is used in Northeast India, Southeast Asia, and the Amazon Basin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Manuring_a_vegetable_garden.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Manuring_a_vegetable_garden.jpg/220px-Manuring_a_vegetable_garden.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Manuring_a_vegetable_garden.jpg/330px-Manuring_a_vegetable_garden.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Manuring_a_vegetable_garden.jpg/440px-Manuring_a_vegetable_garden.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Manuring_a_vegetable_garden.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Spreading manure by hand in Zambia</div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subsistence_farming" title="Subsistence farming">Subsistence farming</a> is practiced to satisfy family or local needs alone, with little left over for transport elsewhere. It is intensively practiced in Monsoon Asia and South-East Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> An estimated 2.5 billion subsistence farmers worked in 2018, cultivating about 60% of the earth's <a href="/wiki/Arable_land" title="Arable land">arable land</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Intensive_farming" title="Intensive farming">Intensive farming</a> involves a low fallow ratio and a high use of inputs (water, fertilizer, pesticide and automation). This type of farming is practiced mainly in developed countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbcFactSheet_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbcFactSheet-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_agriculture">Contemporary agriculture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Contemporary agriculture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Status">Status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Farm_in_Hainan_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Farm_in_Hainan_01.jpg/220px-Farm_in_Hainan_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Farm_in_Hainan_01.jpg/330px-Farm_in_Hainan_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Farm_in_Hainan_01.jpg/440px-Farm_in_Hainan_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Farm_in_Hainan_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>China has the largest agricultural output of any country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNCTAD2017_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNCTAD2017-77">[77]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Beginning in the twentieth century, intensive agriculture increased productivity,  substitutedof synthetic fertilizers and pesticides for labor, water pollution, and farm subsidies. In recent years there has been a backlash against the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_awareness" title="Environmental awareness">environmental effects</a> of conventional agriculture, resulting in the <a href="/wiki/Organic_farming" title="Organic farming">organic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Regenerative_agriculture" title="Regenerative agriculture">regenerative</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_agriculture" title="Sustainable agriculture">sustainable agriculture</a> movements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-motherjones1_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-motherjones1-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> One of the major forces behind this movement has been the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, which first certified <a href="/wiki/Organic_food" title="Organic food">organic food</a> in 1991 and began reform of its <a href="/wiki/Common_Agricultural_Policy" title="Common Agricultural Policy">Common Agricultural Policy</a> (CAP) in 2005 to phase out commodity-linked farm subsidies,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> also known as <a href="/wiki/Decoupling_and_re-coupling" title="Decoupling and re-coupling">decoupling</a>. The growth of organic farming has renewed research in alternative technologies such as <a href="/wiki/Integrated_pest_management" title="Integrated pest management">integrated pest management</a>, selective breeding,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Controlled-environment_agriculture" title="Controlled-environment agriculture">controlled-environment agriculture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Recent mainstream technological developments include <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_food" title="Genetically modified food">genetically modified food</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Demand for non-food biofuel crops,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> development of former farm lands, rising transportation costs, <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>, growing consumer demand in China and India, and <a href="/wiki/Population_growth" title="Population growth">population growth</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> are threatening <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">food security</a> in many parts of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian.co.uk_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-guardian.co.uk-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timesonline.co.uk_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-timesonline.co.uk-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-90">[90]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/International_Fund_for_Agricultural_Development" title="International Fund for Agricultural Development">International Fund for Agricultural Development</a> posits that an increase in <a href="/wiki/Smallholding" title="Smallholding">smallholder agriculture</a> may be part of the solution to concerns about food prices and overall food security, given the favorable experience of Vietnam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Soil_retrogression_and_degradation" title="Soil retrogression and degradation">Soil degradation</a> and diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Stem_rust" title="Stem rust">stem rust</a> are major concerns globally;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> approximately 40% of the world's agricultural land is seriously degraded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> By 2015, the <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_China" title="Agriculture in China">agricultural output of China</a> was the largest in the world, followed by the European Union, India and the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNCTAD2017_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-UNCTAD2017-77">[77]</a></sup> Economists measure the <a href="/wiki/Total_factor_productivity" title="Total factor productivity">total factor productivity</a> of agriculture and by this measure agriculture in the United States is roughly 1.7 times more productive than it was in 1948.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Workforce">Workforce</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Workforce">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Transition_from_agriculture_to_developed_economy.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="838" data-file-width="1069" decoding="async" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Transition_from_agriculture_to_developed_economy.svg/330px-Transition_from_agriculture_to_developed_economy.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Transition_from_agriculture_to_developed_economy.svg/495px-Transition_from_agriculture_to_developed_economy.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Transition_from_agriculture_to_developed_economy.svg/660px-Transition_from_agriculture_to_developed_economy.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Transition_from_agriculture_to_developed_economy.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>On the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Three-sector_theory" title="Three-sector theory">three-sector theory</a>, the proportion of people working in agriculture (left-hard bar in each group, green) falls as an economy becomes more developed.</div></div></div>
<p>Following the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Three-sector_theory" title="Three-sector theory">three-sector theory</a>, the number of people employed in agriculture and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primary_sector" title="Primary sector">primary</a> activities (such as fishing) can be more than 80% in the least developed countries, and less than 2% in the most highly developed countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LaborForce_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-LaborForce-96">[96]</a></sup> Since the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, many countries have made the transition to developed economies, and the proportion of people working in agriculture has steadily fallen. During the 16th century in Europe, for example, between 55 and 75% of the population was engaged in agriculture; by the 19th century, this had dropped to between 35 and 65%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> In the same countries today, the figure is less than 10%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LaborForce_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-LaborForce-96">[96]</a></sup>
At the start of the 21st century, some one billion people, or over 1/3 of the available work force, were employed in agriculture. It constitutes approximately 70% of the global employment of children, and in many countries employs the largest percentage of women of any industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ILO_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-ILO-98">[98]</a></sup> The service sector overtook the agricultural sector as the largest global employer in 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety">Safety</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Safety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ford_Tractor_with_ROPS_bar_fitted.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Ford_Tractor_with_ROPS_bar_fitted.JPG/220px-Ford_Tractor_with_ROPS_bar_fitted.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Ford_Tractor_with_ROPS_bar_fitted.JPG/330px-Ford_Tractor_with_ROPS_bar_fitted.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Ford_Tractor_with_ROPS_bar_fitted.JPG/440px-Ford_Tractor_with_ROPS_bar_fitted.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ford_Tractor_with_ROPS_bar_fitted.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rollover_protection_system" title="Rollover protection system">Rollover protection bar</a> on a mid-20th century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fordson_tractor" title="Fordson tractor">Fordson tractor</a></div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_safety_and_health" title="Agricultural safety and health">Agricultural safety and health</a></div>
<p>Agriculture, specifically <a href="/wiki/Farm" title="Farm">farming</a>, remains a hazardous industry, and farmers worldwide remain at high risk of work-related injuries, lung disease, <a href="/wiki/Noise-induced_hearing_loss" title="Noise-induced hearing loss">noise-induced hearing loss</a>, skin diseases, as well as certain cancers related to chemical use and prolonged sun exposure. On <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrial_agriculture" title="Industrial agriculture">industrialized farms</a>, injuries frequently involve the use of <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_machinery" title="Agricultural machinery">agricultural machinery</a>, and a common cause of fatal agricultural injuries in developed countries is <a href="/wiki/Rollover_protection_structure" title="Rollover protection structure">tractor rollovers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aginjury_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-aginjury-100">[100]</a></sup> Pesticides and other chemicals used in farming can also be hazardous to worker health, and workers exposed to pesticides may experience illness or have children with birth defects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIOSH_pest_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIOSH_pest-101">[101]</a></sup> As an industry in which families commonly share in work and live on the farm itself, entire families can be at risk for injuries, illness, and death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIOSH_Agri_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIOSH_Agri-102">[102]</a></sup> Ages 0–6 may be an especially vulnerable population in agriculture;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WeicheltGorucu2018_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-WeicheltGorucu2018-103">[103]</a></sup> common causes of fatal injuries among young farm workers include drowning, machinery and motor accidents, including with all-terrain vehicles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIOSH_Agri_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-NIOSH_Agri-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WeicheltGorucu2018_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-WeicheltGorucu2018-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>The International Labour Organization considers agriculture "one of the most hazardous of all economic sectors".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ILO_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-ILO-98">[98]</a></sup> It estimates that the annual work-related death toll among agricultural employees is at least 170,000, twice the average rate of other jobs. In addition, incidences of death, injury and illness related to agricultural activities often go unreported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ILO2_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-ILO2-104">[104]</a></sup> The organization has developed the <a href="/wiki/Safety_and_Health_in_Agriculture_Convention,_2001" title="Safety and Health in Agriculture Convention, 2001">Safety and Health in Agriculture Convention, 2001</a>, which covers the range of risks in the agriculture occupation, the prevention of these risks and the role that individuals and organizations engaged in agriculture should play.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ILO_98-2"><a href="#cite_note-ILO-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>In America, agriculture has been identified by the <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_for_Occupational_Safety_and_Health" title="National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health">National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health</a> as a priority industry sector in the <a href="/wiki/National_Occupational_Research_Agenda" title="National Occupational Research Agenda">National Occupational Research Agenda</a> to identify and provide intervention strategies for occupational health and safety issues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
In the European Union, the <a href="/wiki/European_Agency_for_Safety_and_Health_at_Work" title="European Agency for Safety and Health at Work">European Agency for Safety and Health at Work</a> has issued guidelines on implementing health and safety directives in agriculture, livestock farming, horticulture, and forestry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<p>Overall production varies by country as listed.

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crop_cultivation_systems">Crop cultivation systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Crop cultivation systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:An_example_of_slash_and_burn_agriculture_practice_Thailand.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/An_example_of_slash_and_burn_agriculture_practice_Thailand.jpg/220px-An_example_of_slash_and_burn_agriculture_practice_Thailand.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/An_example_of_slash_and_burn_agriculture_practice_Thailand.jpg/330px-An_example_of_slash_and_burn_agriculture_practice_Thailand.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/An_example_of_slash_and_burn_agriculture_practice_Thailand.jpg/440px-An_example_of_slash_and_burn_agriculture_practice_Thailand.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:An_example_of_slash_and_burn_agriculture_practice_Thailand.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slash_and_burn" title="Slash and burn">Slash and burn</a> shifting cultivation, Thailand</div></div></div>
<p>Cropping systems vary among farms depending on the available resources and constraints; geography and climate of the farm; government policy; economic, social and political pressures; and the philosophy and culture of the farmer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAO_FS_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_FS-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PCP_APS_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-PCP_APS-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>Shifting cultivation (or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slash_and_burn" title="Slash and burn">slash and burn</a>) is a system in which forests are burnt, releasing nutrients to support cultivation of annual and then <a href="/wiki/Perennial_plant" title="Perennial plant">perennial</a> crops for a period of several years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CS_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-CS-110">[110]</a></sup> Then the plot is left fallow to regrow forest, and the farmer moves to a new plot, returning after many more years (10–20). This fallow period is shortened if population density grows, requiring the input of nutrients (fertilizer or <a href="/wiki/Manure" title="Manure">manure</a>) and some manual <a href="/wiki/Pest_control" title="Pest control">pest control</a>. Annual cultivation is the next phase of intensity in which there is no fallow period. This requires even greater nutrient and pest control inputs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CS_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-CS-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Intercropping_coconut_n_Tagetes_erecta.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1363" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Intercropping_coconut_n_Tagetes_erecta.jpg/220px-Intercropping_coconut_n_Tagetes_erecta.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Intercropping_coconut_n_Tagetes_erecta.jpg/330px-Intercropping_coconut_n_Tagetes_erecta.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Intercropping_coconut_n_Tagetes_erecta.jpg/440px-Intercropping_coconut_n_Tagetes_erecta.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Intercropping_coconut_n_Tagetes_erecta.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Intercropping" title="Intercropping">Intercropping</a> of <a href="/wiki/Coconut" title="Coconut">coconut</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tagetes_erecta" title="Tagetes erecta">Mexican marigold</a></div></div></div>
<p>Further industrialization led to the use of <a href="/wiki/Monoculture" title="Monoculture">monocultures</a>, when one <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivar</a> is planted on a large acreage. Because of the low <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>, nutrient use is uniform and pests tend to build up, necessitating the greater use of <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a> and fertilizers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PCP_APS_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-PCP_APS-109">[109]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Multiple_cropping" title="Multiple cropping">Multiple cropping</a>, in which several crops are grown sequentially in one year, and <a href="/wiki/Intercropping" title="Intercropping">intercropping</a>, when several crops are grown at the same time, are other kinds of annual cropping systems known as <a href="/wiki/Polyculture" title="Polyculture">polycultures</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CS_110-2"><a href="#cite_note-CS-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Subtropics" title="Subtropics">subtropical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arid" title="Arid">arid</a> environments, the timing and extent of agriculture may be limited by rainfall, either not allowing multiple annual crops in a year, or requiring irrigation. In all of these environments perennial crops are grown (coffee, chocolate) and systems are practiced such as agroforestry. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperateness" title="Temperateness">temperate</a> environments, where ecosystems were predominantly <a href="/wiki/Grassland" title="Grassland">grassland</a> or <a href="/wiki/Prairie" title="Prairie">prairie</a>, highly productive annual farming is the dominant agricultural system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CS_110-3"><a href="#cite_note-CS-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>Important categories of food crops include <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">cereals</a>, legumes, forage, fruits and vegetables.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAO_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAO-111">[111]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Natural_fiber" title="Natural fiber">Natural fibers</a> include <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">cotton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wool" title="Wool">wool</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hemp" title="Hemp">hemp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flax" title="Flax">flax</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Specific crops are cultivated in distinct <a href="/wiki/Growing_region" title="Growing region">growing regions</a> throughout the world. Production is listed in millions of metric tons, based on <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">FAO</a> estimates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAO_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-FAO-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
